B000005E3K|3.0|In the hands of a Master, Mozart's Requiem is kin to Macduff's bellow: \\"Shake off this downy sleep, death's counterfeit, And look on death itself.\\"<br /><br />A fellow reviewer's disparaging comments on Christie in Charpentier caused me to dust off my copy of his K 626. For years it has been sitting there, untouched and unloved. It's time to ratify the original thumbs-down.<br /><br />There is one out-take from Christie's Requiem Aeternam, Kyrie and Dies Irae:  the conductor is emotionally inhibited. He is a blood-brother to Jeggy - so to speak.<br /><br />The Tuba Mirum is wretched. Its trombone opens unsubtly and the singers (particularly the bass) follow its lead. Anachronistically and unconvincingly, Christie slams on the brakes towards the end of the Rex Tremendae. The Confutatis is the greatest movement in the Requiem: it mirrors the Last Judgement. Here, it's inert: there is no smell of brimstone where the \\"just man is barely safe\\". This failure relegates Christie to the billy-goat category.  The Lacrymosa is dutiful rather than elegiac. With his ticket-to-conduct clipped by the Period Practice Taliban, Christie skips through the Hostias as if gathering nuts in May. Disappointingly, one never senses an inner dialogue in the Benedictus: they soloists sing the text aptly and no more. Speaking of which, the soprano Anna Maria Panzarella has a twittery weightless voice that is so beloved by practitioners of period practice but it does bugger all for me. Stutzmann is easier on the ear and makes more of the text itself. I was not overwhelmed by Pregardien, not that the tenor has much to do in this work.<br /><br />The Ave Verum Corpus is not a patch on the famous [[ASIN:B0000040XF Mozart: Exsultate, Jubilate, K. 165; Vesperae solennes de confessore, K. 339; Kyrie in D minor, K. 341; Ave verum corpus, K. 618]]. And De Burgos (among many others) is far preferable in the main work [[ASIN:B000026GD1 Mozart: Requiem]]<br /><br />All in all, this Christie performance is not an 'into-the-crypt' rendition of K 626. As period practice goes, Christie is not an outrider like Harnoncourt or Savall: it's listenable and reverentially performed. Intonation is good. But make no mistake: this is a faux requiem. And Macduff, where are you?not an 'into-the-crypt' rendition of K 626. As period practice goes, Christie is not an outrider like Harnoncourt or Savall: it's listenable and reverentially performed. 1594744769|5.0|As an author, I read a great deal within my chosen genre, to keep myself abreast of what the market is like. Usually, this research does not inspire particularly active interest, and indeed I usually feel like I could do better myself, or that the author has done little more than perpetuate YA sterotypes and indulge in lazy plot construction. (I hate it when I can see the seams in a novel.) In the instance of Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children, however, I was struck by the originality and particular voice of the author.<br /><br />Riggs' characters are clever and well depicted, and the story line has no holes and few shoddy seams. I'm not usually a fan of novels that end as cliff-hangers or obvious \\"Act 1\\"s of a larger series, but I can forgive Riggs for this. At least he rounded out enough of this story to make the ending at least endurable if not satisfying.<br /><br />But of course, the real draw for this book are its peculiar photographs. Applying the oddities of human action in a novel of this sort, to attach lives and a story to the souless, forgotten, two dimentional images of these antique photos, is a stroke of genius. Occationally, the insertion of the photos into the story line seemed a little obvious, if not entirely forced -- I'd hope for a little more finesse in the sequel which is obviously on its way -- but that hardly matters when you take into account the impact these photos have on the reader.<br /><br />This novel has just the right level of creepy, and the photographs bring that home in a way no mere description can convey. To the extent that I found myself waking up from a nightmare this morning, with perfect images of the characters and villains running around in my subconcious. I was not disturbed because the book was unduly gory, or the characters or situation overly disturbing (though the most disturbing character was supposed to be a good guy. Explain to me exactly how any normal non-psychopathic person REALIZES he can rip the hearts out of animals and animate corpses or dolls with them?) But the inclusion of the photographs into the story -- photographs and drawings that would have been disturbing by themselves -- drills the story and characters into your mind. I found myself reminded of some of Edward Gorey's excellent line drawings.<br /><br />The photographs I found the most disturbing, oddly, were not the creepy wild-eyed children or the strange monsterous creatures, but the heart-rending depictions of normal human moments. In particular the photograph entitled \\"This is why,\\" could, even by itself, make your heart bleed. Well done Ransom. May your sequels be as clever.te corpses or dolls with them?) I thought Beth was supposed to be a Janis disciple--well, where's the blues rock of Janis Joplin? I didn't find much of it on here. I did find a lot of ballads with Kevin Shirley's trademark insomnia-killing, lush orchestration backgrounds. On the last track, Hart sounds like she's imitating Sarah McLaughlin more than Janis--zzzzzzzzz. And Joe's guitar playing is too restrained, I think. He only kinda lets loose on one song, and the other tracks just don't sound like Joe. Sure, shredding would have been overkill on most of these tracks, so that's why I wish they would have picked some tracks that lent more to Joe's enormous guitar talent. And the sound of his guitar is murky and distant, like he's playing in a big empty room three doors down the hall--not good at all.  The Billie Holiday song is boring too, sounding too much like actual Billie Holiday recordings. Why not update the sound somewhat--just too dated. If you're going to cover a song for a CD, it seems to make sense to do something different with it and make it better, at least in some ways, rather than stick to the original sound. What I DID like was Beth's impersonation of Etta James--great stuff! She really nailed Etta's vocal style, and I thought the legend herself was guest-singing when I first heard her. \\"Something's Really Got A Hold On Me\\" is a great song, but the band screwed around with the chord changes at the end of the chorus and kind of ruined it. I'm all for changing the music if it makes it sound better, but I think in this case it definitely did not. And again, they could have if they wanted to.  Since this was a covers-only album, the field was wide open, and they could have picked some better material to record. 0756405890|5.0|As the first book in the Kingkiller Chronicle, \\"The Name of the Wind\\" does a good job of setting the stage and developing the characters that will then (I'm assuming) play a bigger role in the two following books.<br /><br />The interesting thing about this book - well, the Chronicle in general - is that it is split between two different time frames. In the `present' time, the main character is an inn-keeper hiding on the edge of civilization. He is fairly happy in this new role...except that deep within himself there is a longing for the glory days of old. So he begins to tell the story of this life to a scribe who wanders into the inn on accident....<br /><br />This is where the second time frame comes in - the time frame of the past. It is in this time frame that takes up the lion share of the book as it is full of daring adventures, romance and history (the history is important as it helps one to understand the new world created by Rothfuss for his characters).<br /><br />Being a fantasy novel, there is magic and the like - only in this series, the magic is more along the lines of Clarke's third law:<br /><br />\\"Any sufficiently advanced technology is indistinguishable from magic.\\"<br /><br />In Rothfuss' universe, this means that the `magic' used by the characters are based upon `scientific' laws and principles. For example if a character wanted to lift an object using `sympathy' (one of the types of `magic'), the user would have to use enough force to lift that object regardless if they were actually touching it or not. In addition, matter can not be created nor destroyed - thereby removing some of the more unbelievable uses of magic (as opposed to `believable' magic) promoted by other fantasy books.<br /><br />All in all, \\"The Name of the Wind\\" by Patrick Rothfuss was a good book - great characters, great plot, perhaps a tad long...but overall alright.<br /><br />Nope, the saddest part about the book is that fact that book two was just released this year - meaning that I will have to wait three to four years for the last book of the trilogy. :(ok of the trilogy. :(

0060734191|5.0|I honestly cannot recommend Garth Nix's Old Kingdom series highly enough - it has been more than ten years since I was introduced to the first book in this series, and yet I have remained hooked ever since.  Part of the reason for this is the rich world building and highly intriguing mythos that Nix has created. For instance, the Old Kingdom (in which these books are predominantly set) is a dark, mysterious realm in which magic abounds. Bordering the Old Kingdom to the south is the country of Ancelstierre - it is the antithesis of the Old Kingdom, being driven by technology and completely free of magic (as a matter of fact, it somewhat resembles England from the 1920s!). The two lands are divided by a great Wall - even time flows differently between the two sides. One may, for instance, cross from Ancelstierre into the Old Kingdom, and find that spring has mysteriously changed to winter!  In addition, Death in itself is a realm of sorts, being comprised of a river flowing through a number of precincts (the river \\"Styx\\" from ancient Greek mythology came to mind as I was reading this). Upon dying, a spirit must pass through the nine Gates separating each precinct before finally passing beyond into true Death. However, the dead have an somewhat irksome habit of not always staying dead! What's more, when they come back to life, their natures are often corrupted and they seek to take over the bodies of the living. Thus, a lineage of anti-necromancers, known as \\"Abhorsens\\", have the task of putting these violent spirits to rest and sending them back into the river of death.  However, I feel that the main focus and strength of the series is the characters themselves, who are brilliantly written. All too often, the stars of fantasy series suffer from irritating character cliches (such as \\"Mary Sue-isms\\"). Not so in these books! The star of the first novel Sabriel, for instance, is typically strong-willed, decisive and intelligent, but also highly realistic - her actions andher vulnerabilities are very believable and, well, human. Other wonderfully likeable characters include Lirael, reluctant Abhorsen-in-Waiting Sameth, a talking dog (known as \\"The Disreputable Dog\\") - and of course, who could forget the wonderfully snarky cat, Mogget?   The first novel \\"Sabriel\\" is centred on the titular character, who is summoned away from her boarding school in Ancelstierre to come to the aid of her Abhorsen father and defeat an evil spirit that has emerged from the realm of Death to plague the Old Kingdom. The second novel, \\"Lirael\\", is set over a decade after the events of Sabriel - it focuses mostly on teenager Lirael who lives on a glacier with a race of Seers known as the Clayr. The co-star of this book is the reluctant young Sameth, prince of the Old Kingdom and also the Abhorsen-in-Waiting. Both Lirael and Sameth are ill at ease with their fortunes - Lirael is somewhat of an outcast, being the only Clayr without the gift of the Sight, and Sameth happens to be morbidly afraid of the dead! However, they are soon to find their true strengths, as they are pitted against a sinister new evil threatening the realm. The story comes to a stunning conclusion in the final novel, \\"Abhorsen.\\" I won't spoil anything - just read it!   I must say that Nix has a wonderful way of writing - there's obviously a lot of depth to his creation, but no tedium and unnecessary details, being reasonably fast-paced. Once you read one page, you are simply gripped by the adventure unfolding before your eyes.   All in all, a spell-binding journey awaits new readers!

1400064287|5.0|The SUCCESs. Not the word that counts its literal meaning, but that invisible, intangible theory where we are able to express, deliver, and stick ideas to others. In this revealing book, you will be introduced to the six ingredients designed specifically to make ideas sticky, and let me deliver what I caught from this eye-opening book.<br /><br /> Others may experience over time they develop habits that slowly erode their mind's sensitivity. The inevitable pain and disappointment of moments such as delivering your ideas at a business meeting or a conference have caused you to set up walls around your mind. Much of this is understandable. But, there's no way around the truth: your mind is out of tune with confidence it was created to maintain. As we live in community, communication is the way for us to feel the unity. The book is even greater because the authors, Chip and Dan Heath, apply their SUCCESs theory onto practical situation to help readers understand more clearly. Without the SUCCESs rule, some kinds of communications might ease our conscience temporarily but would do nothing to expose the deeper secrets we carry and deliver. And, it might be the secrets that keep our minds in turmoil. Worse, this kind of communication could actually fuel destructive behavior rather than curb it. The rules the authors explain in this book might seem the things you would feel that you already know. But, these are the things you could easily ignore. The book is a great reference to keep you on succeeding the efficient deliverability of your ideas.<br /><br />Chapter summary<br />Chapter1: Simple<br />When you needed to deliver your message in a brief and compact way, how would you prepare to deliver it to your audiences or readers? Simplicity is the key and first step to make a message sticky to others. Making it simple does not mean that you need to bring out your most important idea. It is critical to find the core. According to the authors, \\"finding the core isn't synonymous with communicating the core.\\" But, that simplicity must come with its value. Like the metaphor of a company for the employees to be encouraged, your message needs to be simple and important to make your message remain not just in your mind but others as well.<br /><br />Chapter2: Unexpected<br />\\"We can't demand attention. We must attract it\\" says the authors in the book. In order to grab people's attention, your message may be attractive with unexpectedness. Breaking a pattern could be one way. For example, the old emergency siren was too monotonic to stimulate our sensory systems and therefore failing to attract our attention. As the siren gets systematically and audibly improved, people hear much brighter and more stimulating sound and therefore being aware of some situation. In order to catch people's attention, you need to break the ordinary patterns. 030788743X|5.0|Is it the lure of nostalgia or simply the epic feel of Ready Player One that makes it so deliciously entertaining? It is perhaps, a bit of both. Ernest Cline playfully reminisces while offering up a heady eighties banquet in his futuristic piece about a quest to end all quests that jet-propels the minds and hearts of readers from the very first page.<br /><br />I can still remember the very first time I saw Star Wars and Tron, the first time I delved into books that epically moved me...Watership Down, The Hobbit, Neuromancer, Holy Fire, Heart of Darkness, even Harry Potter...diving into Ready Player One, with my haptic gloves, visor, and OASIS immersion rig was no different-an absolutely electrifying and unforgettable experience.<br />Cline manages to convey a bit of prescience in his portrayal of the world that Wade Watts lives in...a virtual world that is more comforting than a bleak future, where millions grow up in the OASIS, through its schools, its libraries, and its simulated cities and towns-all digitally recreated-its seamless worlds-within-worlds offering up a never-ending adventure. Sound familiar?<br /><br />Clines' futuristic grail quest takes off when the creator of the OASIS and owner of Gregarious Simulation Systems James Halliday dies, thrusting the entire planet into a frenzy (much in the same way Steve Jobs' demise did).<br />Within the OASIS, Halliday has programmed clues to unraveling a treasure-his treasure-roughly two hundred and forty billion dollars' worth and a controlling interest of the most powerful company on the planet. All are at stake and up for grabs. One only needs to have an excellent knowledge of the eighties, the background of Halliday's life memorized, and an unbeatable strategy to unravel the scavenger hunt-like clues hidden within the OASIS.<br /><br />Cline plays up his inexhaustible eighties references, as Wade Watts does his pasty-white-best to unravel the clues to three keys left by his idol Halliday-whether it is acting out the scenes in War Games, playing Joust, Dungeons of Daggorath, or playing a perfect game of Pac Man (which is still on my bucket list) on top of competitively racing against thousands of gunters bent on finding the keys for themselves. At their heels an evil multinational company lurks in the wings willing to do anything, include kill, to get at all three keys.<br /><br />Cline manages to craft a formidable opponent out of the Sixers, those cretinous suits from innovative Online Industries (IOI) bent on sussing out the keys for their own motely gain and the inevitable control and monetization of the OASIS.<br />With the emergence of the Sixers, Wade must pit his smarts and knowledge against them, and band together his friends and fellow gunters worldwide. With Wade's selfless perseverance to save the spirit of the OASIS from the Sixers, Ready Player One becomes ceases to be a quest and morphs into a worldwide mission.<br /><br />Instead of the billions at stake, the very life blood of the OASIS is threatened and the unified front that Wade and his teammates drum up conjures up visions of every epic fictional battle fought: the war of Middle Earth, the Battle of Endor, Luke Skywalker launching his torpedo into the Death Star's reactor...to Cline's Battle of Chthonia and Castle Anorak all thrust the reader from apathy to become a part of something greater. Cline has managed to imaginatively deliver the most essential ingredients to a great story or movie in a shiny, new, wrapper: charity, hope and faith.<br /><br />Ready Player One is striking, unstoppable and epically good. There are so many good and genuine aspects to Cline's novel that I don't even know where to start.<br /><br />Cline's peek at the future is believable at times with a hint of wry humor (i.e. the Portland Avenue Stacks where Wade lives...is freakishly comical...and yet just a bit sad) Who could imagine poverty-stricken families scraping out a meager existence in RVs stacked high in the sky? Visually it's like the Jetson's in HELL or the great garbage avalanche of 2505 in Idiocracy (2006).<br /><br />As is his reference to a C-section (cutting an OASIS user out of their apartment and haptic chair) nearly made me roll on the floor laughing and Wades' stint as an IOI indentured Tech Support employee was insanely good, (almost as good as his maniacal plot to somehow crack the hold of the Orb of Osuvox over Castle Anorak).<br /><br />After reading Ready Player One FIVE times, I have to say without a doubt it's been the best book I've read all year. Hopefully, Ernest Cline will channel Yoda's last words \\"...there is another Skyw-...\\"  and deliver an equally geek-worthy sequel.<br /><br />Bravo! I found it hard to believe Victoria was able to start up her own wildly successful flower shop while pregnant, relatively friendless, and living in one of the most expensive cities in the U.S. The nursing passage was well done, but it seemed a little out of place with the fantastical nature of the rest of the book. In the end, all the loose ends were tied up in a way that I found unrealistic and trite. A story about a foster child should be a little more savvy, in my opinion. I think it would have been possible to create a happy ending with some lessons learned, but without doing a disservice to all the readers who know just how the garden really grows.

There is considerable hoopla thrown in the direction of this trilogy.  The Swedish films made quite a splash here in the states, not to mention their homeland.  `The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo' film made a big dent here, even when awards started to roll around (for a foreign language film to get nominated for awards in the US, it means something) and the sting of the controversy surrounding the death of the author, Stieg Larsson, continues to loom over the overall impact and reception of the franchise.  There is a lot of critical words used in detraction of these books and their subsequent films (which are currently being remade by American author filmmaker David Fincher), but where there is negativity there are equal amounts of praise and adoration being lumped on them as well.<br /><br />The truth of the matter is that `The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo' isn't going away anytime soon.<br /><br />My personal feeling about this novel (I have yet to read the second and third novels, but they are on my reading list) is one of overall satisfaction.  There are some obvious faults here, most I chuck up to the translation (being translated into English from the original Swedish text could have caused the wording to suffer a bit).  There is some dialog that feels cheesy and underwritten and some wording that feels amateurish, which is off-putting when the bulk of the novel is so compelling.  I also felt that Larsson had an issue with editing himself.  He tried so hard to flesh out every character and every backstory and every side plot that he wound up writing long passages that took the reader out to the immediate action and into another world almost.  He wrote it well (honestly, those pages just fly by) but I can't help but think that there were a lot of details he didn't need to include.  That said, when you are finished you certainly feel as if you know THE WHOLE STORY, and I understand (especially from having seen all three films) that those details will come in handy as I delve into the second and third novels.<br /><br />So I guess I can't fault him too much for that.<br /><br />The story here, or at least the central story, focuses on a missing persons investigation.  Journalist Mikael Blomkvist, who has just been sentenced to serve a prison term for libel, is hired by an aging millionaire, Henrik Vanger, to find his missing niece Harriet.  She has been missing since the 60's and the case was close (cold) decades ago, but Henrik has been haunted by her disappearance and now that he is near death he is determined to find her.  He is convinced that someone within his family (he hates them all) has murdered her.  Mikael is skeptical, but the offer eventually persuades him (his interest is certainly pricked) but as he gets deeper into discovering the truth he finds himself way in over his head.  Then we have Lisbeth Salander, an introverted analyst working for a security company who is hired to do a background analysis of Blomkvist and finds herself intrigued.  She is tormented by her own past (carefully guarded in this particular book) and so she winds up taking part in unlocking this mystery alongside Mikael.  Salander is an odd girl, but one that is complex and intriguing.  She dresses in Goth and looks like a boy and yet she possesses an edge that makes her sexy.  She acts rather slow and yet she is far from dumb, and while she vents her frustrations in often violent and confrontational ways, she is undeniably vulnerable and emotionally wounded.<br /><br />Honestly, the universal consumption with this character is well deserved; she is that unique.<br /><br />While it has been noted that certain aspects of this prose lack originality, and the general consensus is that this is a generic crime novel, there is one aspect of this book that cannot so easily be generalized; Salander.  She is the heart and soul of this book and she makes her presence known with sheer strength and identity.  Say what you will about the overall `impact' here, but I must admit that there are few novels that have engulfed me the way that this one has.<br /><br />I couldn't put it down.aspect of this book that cannot so easily be generalized; Salander.  She is the heart and soul of this book and she makes her presence known with sheer strength and identity.  Say what you will about the overall `impact' here, but I must admit that there are few novels that have engulfed me the way that this one has.  I couldn't put it down.

B005AVIZ6U|5.0|Jack Reacher is back in Lee Child's sixteenth book [[ASIN:B004P8JPS6 The Affair: A Reacher Novel (Jack Reacher)]].  The book details Jack Reacher's final days as a military policemen in the army, while investigating a murder in a small town called Carter Crossing, near an army base, Fort Kelham.  It is an interesting story, although a little inplausable at times, but still greatly enjoyable, especially for those that are keen Reacher fans.  I wouldn't recommend this book as a starting point for people interested in the series; a prior knowledge of [[ASIN:B000OZ0NXA Killing Floor (Jack Reacher, No. 1)]] is needed to greater appreciate some of the background (though relatively unimportant) points of the story.  I also enjoyed the references to how some of his quirky behaviour that is evident in older books came to be (for instance his love for the foldable toothbrush), as well as how he thought his life was going to be like outside of the army. As for the writing style, it is Lee Child as we know him.  The story flows wonderfully from chapter to chapter, all in the first person perspective of Reacher.  This isn't anything new from Lee Child, but it is refreshing, as most books in the series have been in the third person.    Overall, The Affair is an extremely enjoyable addition to the Jack Reacher series.  It may not be the best story in the series, but it is well written, and will be interesting to those that have always wondered how it came to be that Reacher left the army.

B0013FDM7E|3.0|As with almost any literary adaptation, fans of the source material in search of a literal interpretation will probably find a lot to fault in this Will Smith vehicle, particularly in its neutered take on the story's conclusion. All of the original's deeper underlying meanings are still there, buried in the actions and expressions of the transfigured creatures Smith does his best to avoid, but without a big reveal to lay all the cards on the table they're too easily shrugged off or overlooked altogether. That positions this as, basically, a hybrid summer blockbuster / suspense-laden monster movie with a better than usual premise. In this role it fares well - Smith portrays the terrifying isolation of the last sane man on Earth with passion and depth, leaving memories of his less-inspired earlier work far in the past. He's come a long way from Independence Day or even Hancock, and surprisingly little of that has the more stern-faced, serious script to thank. Legend's computer effects are a different story: while exceptional at rendering an overgrown, deserted NYC skyline, they feel excessive and overwhelming when it come to the creatures themselves. Though it may not live up to the lofty expectations of its namesake, this is hardly a complete write-off. In fact, as mass market action pictures go it's unusually smart, expertly paced and generally contemplative. A pity it didn't have the balls to stick with that original ending, though, as I think it would have been much more universally appreciated.

The first 12 comics see Batman on a wild goose chase from Gotham City to Metropolis to find out the identity of Hush - and it is not your typical wild goose chase from a good detective novel. No, this is a disparate mesh of story lines and characters, most of them unrelated, trying to gel together a dramatic story. It has failed terribly.<br /><br />If I were to analyze how Hush has failed compared to Jeph Loeb's earlier work, I'd say that he was probably `star struck'. Star struck by Jim Lee/Scott Williams. 0066214122|5.0|In evolution, the little guys always win. In his famous previous works, Crichton focused on the big creatures. This time, it's the little guys turn.  For dinosaurs, the evolution process operates slowly over hundreds of thousands of years. But what about microscopic organisms?  In quickly reproducing life forms, like bacteria, evolution could potentially occur in a matter of weeks. If evolutionary change occurs incrementally from one generation to the next, wouldn't it be an evolutionary advantage to have more generations in a given period?  In other words, all else being equal, the more generations you have, the more quickly evolution happens and the more quickly simple organisms can become complex.<br /><br />Prey is a stark reminder of how quickly fortunes can change in the tech business and a cautionary tale for unbridled research. As humans, we have limits to our intelligence and problem solving. We can only make so many incremental advances over a period of time. Crichton proposes a new way of making quantum leaps in our technological advances. Crichton uses `emergent behavior' as the impetus for transcending human limitations in problem solving. He surmises a complex adaptive system model of a natural phenomenon where simple structures working together in a dynamic process. For example, individual cells interact to form differentiated body parts, ants interact to form colonies, neurons interact to form intelligent systems, and people interact to form social networks.  Emergent behavior is comparable to the infinite monkey theorem, where an infinite number of monkeys hitting keys at random on a typewriter keyboard for an infinite amount of time will almost surely type a given text, such as the complete works of William Shakespeare. But who has an infinite number of monkeys? How about an infinite number of manufactured nanobots that demonstrate emergent behavior over many generations despite limited memory and limited shelf-life? The key for getting a bunch of dim-witted particles to act in concert is the concept of predator-prey dynamics. In Prey, an infinite number of nanobots can use simple algorithms as rules and basic swarm behavior as a model to problem solve their way from dust in the wind to the top of the food chain. Crichton provides an appreciation of the highly organized, orderly behavior that emerges from systems composed out \\"agents\\" behaving in accord with very simple rules. However, he also heeds warning to venture capitalists who exploit the power of human reason through the science and technology. For Crichton, the actual laws of nature always lie beyond the control of human beings while human greed and curiosity lead to destruction and chaos.d particles to act in concert is the concept of predator-prey dynamics. B0028MBKVG|5.0|I read \\"Born to Run\\" more than a year ago in hardcover (rather than Kindle, which is my new medium of choice)and am just now getting around to writing a review. The fact that McDougall's work remains on my mind after all this time attests to its quality and impact. McDougall has become known largely as prophet for barefoot running, but I believe that understates the depth and merit of what he brings to the reader, runner and non-runner alike.  I've been an avid runner through most of my adult life and I've long thought there are important philosophical and spiritual dimensions to running that the running magazines largely miss. By examining the historical experience and perspective of the Tarahumara Indians, MacDougall illuminates new angles and purpose, and ones that have since enriched and informed my own runnning experience---not because I've abandoned traditional running in favor of the barefoot variety (I haven't, although I have started to do more shoeless beach running in the summer) but because running, at its very best, involves transcendance above the ordinary and the constant press of mortality.  I congratulate McDougall for a path-breaking and original book and recommend it to anyone who runs or is considering starting---without or without shoes.

0385534639|5.0|Not everyone will love this book like I did. It is imagery heavy, and impatient readers who aren't able to deal with the sometimes extravagant prose may find themselves losing interest quickly. However, the language is beautiful and the world well described, so if you're the type of reader who wants to truly SEE the world of the author's imagination down to the olfactory stimulation, then this is a book that I can doubly recommend for you. If you are like me, and somewhere in between, it is still definitely worth checking out.  This is a modern love story, and tastefully done. It is NOT a cheesy romance novel, with drawn out scenes of \\"mush\\" if you'll forgive the expression. Instead, Morgenstern chose to focus on the world she was building, the cast of interesting characters, and bringing you the reader into the circus with some imaginative second person writing pieces interspersed among the books sections.   I have read a review where someone complained about not being able to tell who to \\"root for.\\" Apparently to them there were no clear lines of good/evil. I find this argument silly. Good and evil is not always clearly defined. However most readers should have no problem deciding for themselves who is in the right, who is in the wrong, who the victims are, and how evil is never done for the sake of evil. It is in fact the LACK of obvious ill intention that makes these characters so interesting. Evil is typically banal when you first meet it. E.g. Hitler didn't rise to power by being an obvious evil madman, but now, he seems to be a representation of evil incarnate in popular culture today, and for good reason.  It is this \\"realistic\\" flavor to the novel that kept the characters three dimensional and believable. Realistic, as far as a fantasy novel is concerned with realism obviously.  The novel is much more than a love story. It is a story about two approaches to the world, it is a story about free will, and it is, above all else, a story about magic. I loved theworld crafted by Morgenstern's words, and her concept of magic stands out as unique. I especially enjoyed the dynamic of the two main characters' diametrically opposed methods of employing the abilities they have.  If you like fantasy and magic, a well written love story (which are in my opinion far too rare), and you can either tolerate (or relish depending on your personality) the flower description, this book is a definite must. It is an original work, and well worth the read. I can see the potential for great discussions coming from the questions raised by Morgenstern. That alone makes the book worth a read.

If you would like futher validation on the dangers of wheat/gluten grains - \\"Celiac Disease, the Hidden Epidemic\\" by Green & Jones\\", might be helpful.     That our most cherished food is also ONE of the primary causes of ALL of our modern diseases may be distressing to many... the scientific evidence exists and is well worth your attention.  ALL modern diseases (cardiovascular, metabolic/diabetic/hormonal, cancers, automimmune) are strongly related to systemic inflammation.  The \\"cause\\" of that inflammation has been elusive.  Probably due to the \\"wild goose chase\\" that we've been on due to the ever popular \\"fad\\" notion that \\"fat\\" particularly \\"saturated fat\\" is bad for us.  Unfortunately, the healthy saturated animal or plant fat was probably the one thing that was protecting our systems from the onslaught of increasing grains, legumes and carbohydrates in our diet. Getting off all grains & beans and limiting my carbs to 200 kcals/day has worked beautifully for 2 years.  Yes, low carbohydrate - the issues in systemic inflammation are directly related to an interplay of nutritional factors. We can survive but the current health status is quite indicative of the lack of tolerance. So, lots of ribeye, pork chops, cheese, butter & cheese cake (no crust) and vegetable (cooked in butter) AND fermented foods (kimchee, live yogurt).  Oh yeah, even though I was thin, I had a bit of a spare tire, despite lots of good exercise - that's gone too. And, I exercise less now than before. As I plowed through that set I realized I'd missed the boat on Pipers and Meddle, two unqualifiedly great records, and I liked songs I hadn't heard before on other albums up to and including The Wall, another \\"okay double album that would have been a great single album.\\"  I was somewhat disappointed by the sound on OBTW, except for Pipers and Dark Side, which were recent remasters. Other bands' catalogues had been reissued with proper, sometimes spectacular, sonic improvements over earlier CD versions.  However I've now had the opportunity to compare OBTW to the Discovery Box (via MP3s), and I'm surprised to report that overall I greatly prefer the OBTW remasterings.  My general impression is that the Discovery set has been remastered a bit louder and with the bottom end boosted, and more often than not this isn't beneficial to the music.  There's a clarity and balance to the OBTW versions that's sometimes lost in 2011.  I can't say if it's compression, but the Discovery set doesn't sound like it's meant to be listened to on a good stereo or through good headphones, my preference.  Pipers and Dark Side sound pretty terrific in 2011, but the 2006 or 2007 reissues sounded great as well.  Saucerful of Secrets, never a great sounding LP, may have been improved in the new set, but just.  2009 Beatles remasters this set ain't.  The OBTW packaging is great, and I'm glad I don't have reason to ditch the set.  The Discovery set is a missed opportunity.

0307946711|5.0|Nick Hornby was right--this book is \\"fantastically readable.\\" I'm a literature snob and I have encountered few books as instantly captivating as this one. Sure, Emma and Dexter aren't extremely likable characters, but that's precisely their charm: they are profoundly HUMAN characters. Nicholls's portrayal of these two people is completely unsentimental, which is one of my favorite things about his writing. He is utterly truthful and always a shade sarcastic, and you get the impression that the author himself doesn't suffer fools. I was weeping for the last 50-60 pages (it's my own fault for reading it while pregnant), and my husband kept teasing me by asking me if the plot had taken this or that shmaltzy turn, which it never had. I felt completely un-manipulated while reading this story, which is something rare when reading love stories (although I wouldn't reduce this story to falling under that particular category). I have little patience with popular fiction that seeks to tweak my heartstrings. I always maintain that writers should simply tell the story, and if they tell it with enough style and honesty, my heartstrings will be duly tweaked. This book is one of the funniest I've ever read, and also one of the saddest, and it helped that I kept picturing Emma as the divine Anne Hathaway and Dex as the lovely, lovely Jim Sturgess. (I haven't seen the movie, but I love both of those actors.) Full disclosure: I also happen to be a lot like Emma--a rather politically-charged, artsy-fartsy feminist, so I was especially drawn to her character. I could go on and on, but I won't. It's just a clever, \\"fantastically readable\\" book and I'm looking forward to reading David Nicholls's other work.

0807014273|5.0|In his Foreword to MAN'S SEARCH FOR MEANING, Rabbi Harold Kushner writes: \\"If a book has one passage, one idea with the power to change a personal life that alone justifies reading it.\\" I read this book in 1961, reread in 1991 and I have just finished reading it again to get a better understanding of it.  At the age of 85, I am searching for the meaning for my actual survival of five Nazi camps. I bore witness how people were capable to brave the worst conceivable conditions. I do not recall many nihilists among us prisoners, predominantly pious Jews; our omnipresent suffering had meaning. For most of us, zest for life and trust in God didn't diminish. We were daydreamers of pleasant happenings in the past and wishful happenings in the future. Frankl believes that neither pleasure nor power, but the quest for meaning in life is essential. He quotes  Nietzsche's maxim \\"He who has a WHY to live, can bear with almost any HOW.\\"<br /><br /> I shared my life story, mainly my predicament during the Holocaust, with over 700 audiences. I deem it to be my mission; it gives a meaning to my suffering. While my story is primarily about suffering and losses, Victor Frankl's first part of the book is primarily about his strength and will to survive. I have often been asked:\\"What kept you going? Why did you strive to stay alive? How could you survive when so many others haven't?\\"  I have no clear cut answer; it might have been a combination of factors and luck was perhaps one of them. I do know that despite the suffering I strove to survive. The desire and hope to be reunited with my family gave me the courage to suffer.<br /><br />Frankl, an accomplished psychiatrist before the war, seems to have psychiatric mediums. His therapeutic doctrine known as logotherapy (Logo\\" from the Greek denotes, \\"meaning,\\" and therapy is helping people with their neuroses) implies that it is imperative to find meaning for life. He believed that people cannot avoid suffering but they can choose how to cope with it and to find meaning in it. Hope should never be given up; Frankl quotes Otto von Bismarck (1815-1898) \\"Life is like being at the dentist. You always think that the worst is still to come, and yet it is over already.\\" Frankl survived Auschwitz, and three other concentration camps; this was a miracle and a blessing to us. In this book written six months after liberation Frankl writes: \\"The condemned man, immediately before his execution, gets the illusion that he might be reprieved at the very last minute. We, too, clung to shred of hope (p.10).\\"<br /><br />During the Holocaust, I had been subjected to unimaginable acts of extreme wickedness, as reflected in my autobiography From a Name to A Number. I thought that I have seen it all, apparently not. Frankl's book brings to light horrific situations that I have not heard of or read about. His story contributes to the grand, but incomplete, mosaic of Holocaust history. There are still stories to be told, to be written. Once we Holocaust survivors are no longer among the living, our stories, the importance of their intrinsic lessons, should be read and reread. Man's Search for Meaning is a contribution of monumental proportions to the Holocaust legacy. I am grateful to Frankl for enlightening me and others, who have read the 12 million copies, in twenty-four languages, of this book.<br /><br />Frankl had an option, in 1942, to emigrate to the U.S. and thus save his and his wife's life. He had chosen to let his visa lapse, because he didn't want to leave his aging parents in Austria. Consequently, his parents and his wife perished in the Holocaust while incarcerated Viktor Frankl suffered. He found meaning in his suffering. Abiding by the fifth Commandment Honor your Father and Mother was meaningful to Frankl. It is indicative how Frankl's character and compassionate soul enveloped his philosophy.d to find meaning in it. Since the case opens and angle is fixed on flat surface like a desk or table, the player must adjust the height if sitting on a chair. All in all, this is an innovative product and probably the best solution for the road warriors to date. Few things the user will need to adjust but it's remarkable to be able to take a PS3 on the road with a beautiful and bright screen. If I'm to take it on a long road trip, I'll certain consider a portable generator or battery with a 600 watt inverter!
B00005JMJ4|5.0|This movie vividly captures the feeling of being depaysee (out of one's familiar surroundings). The main characters are both lost in Japan, unmoored and lonely, tired, unable to sleep, jet-lagged and confused. Coppola captures this ethos perfectly. The setting is magnificent- a luxurious but cold hotel in Tokyo and the lights/sounds/swirling/frenetic activity in the streets below. Bill Murray nails the jaded Bob and his midlife crisis perfectly. Scarlett Johansson's Charlotte is just realizing that her marriage is not all she expected it to be and that no marriage can provide her with the life direction she is seeking. Charlotte and Bob are both intelligent and highly attuned to the nonsense (e.g. Charlotte's husband's self-absorption, the inanity spewed by the actress Kelly, the artiness of the crew directing the commercial) that surrounds them. High above the Tokyo streets, both protagonists are trapped in their luxurious but cold setting and the two forge an ambiguous, tender bond. It is to Coppola's credit that the bond remains ambiguous to the end (what did they say to one another in the final scene?) and that the movie avoids the traps that could so easily have ensued had the two had allowed the obvious attraction between them to become explicitly sexual. Coppola allows Bob and Charlotte's story to unfold in its own messy,goofy, unclassifiable way. The scenes involving the filming of the whiskey commercial are hysterical. The scene where Murray sings \\"More than this\\" at the Karaoke bar perfectly captures the film's spirit: unpolished, ambiguous, heartfelt and ironic.

B002VKB0M4|4.0|I love French cinema and Paris - but did not have high hopes for this film.  I rented it because I was missing Paris and could tell that the film would have great shots of Paris- and it did.    I have never cared for Binoche.  I found the beginning of the film to be hackneyed and trite.  The character of the professor was off-putting and a bit creepy.  Nevertheless- by the end of the film, I was won over and I think the film is good but not great.  One of my favorite activities (shared by many visitors to Paris) in Paris is watching the life swirl around me on the streets and in the surrounding houses.  This film captures this experience.  We are brought into the interlocking worlds of a number of characters whose lives intersect in ways they do and do not perceive.  Although there are some truly clunky aspects (the affair between student and prof was not credible, the young man's illness seemed to be conveyed in an unconvincing manner, the last-minute intersection of the professor and the dying man, the motorcycle accident, the fashionistas in the warehouse) of this conceit in the film, the melange is ultimately messy, unresolved, and poignant.  One is left with a true appreciation for the people whose lives touch our own.  Some of the best scenes:  the small-minded owner of a boulangerie  the party scene (what great dancing)  the father-to-be  the cruelty of the young student  the tenderness between the fruit seller and Elise  Elise and her children  The last scene- where the dying man looks upon his nieces and nephew, says good bye to this sister, and takes a cab to the hosptial- was just like the film iself- hackneyed, trite,and clunky in places ---but ultimately true-to-life, messy, unresolved, poignant and somewhat magical.

015603008X|5.0|Daniel Keyes weaves a touching and poignant tale of the human condition around the life of a boy who is mentally disabled and offered a second chance. Keyes tackles a difficult topic with poise, respect and thoughtfulness. The book jacket of this version gives away the plot bookends, to some extent, and yet it's the biographical journey of Charlie Gordon that matters most.  The story quickly becomes fascinating and Charlie's progress is measured through almost flawless pacing. Keyes embraces the full scope of his material and keeps it believable and unpredictable throughout the heart of the novel. Charlie Gordon feels real. Some supporting characters are bland, but they highlighted others that are more memorable: my favorite was Winslow.  This book was physically challenging! I found myself pacing at one point. At various times, my heart raced and pounded. Eyes swelled or wrinkled. Lips smiled, cheered and grimaced. My hands roamed my face looking for something, anything, to scratch. I ran the emotional gamut. And I lost sleep.     As someone opposed to all medical testing/research on other animals, I was unsure what message the book would send on this issue. In fact, the subject is never directly approached. To avoid any spoilers, I'll step off this soapbox by saying that the apathy or indifference displayed here by an author who has all other angles carefully considered is what left me most disappointed.  Flowers from Algernon is a fairly quick read that digs below the surface and leaves a lasting impression; it's a remarkable achievement on so many levels. When Charlie asks \\"Why am I always looking at life through a window?\\" it reminded me of the Dalai Lama's \\"Live a good, honorable life. Then when you get older and think back, you'll be able to enjoy it a second time\\". I hope it applies to Charlie Gordon, too.

0525423281|5.0|Anna and the French Kiss is one of my favorite recent contemporary reads so I was eagerly anticipating the companion book's release. Happily, Stephanie Perkins pulled off a great follow up to Anna. Even with a new cast of cast of characters, the feel of the story is just as charming and captivating and left me completely satisfied.<br /><br />Lola is a quirky and flawed yet endearing main character. She's confident and outgoing and likes to have fun with her clothing choices and dress up in wigs and her own fashion creations.  She's 17 and lives in a San Francisco Victorian with her two dads, and has a part time job and a loyal best friend. Her love life is heating up with her rocker boyfriend Max, and things are going pretty well until her old crush Cricket Bell moves back next door and then things start to get complicated.<br /><br />Part of the book's charm is due to the unconventional characters. The main character Lola isn't perfect, and her decisions can be rash and infuriating at times, but I liked that she seemed authentic. The supporting characters are fully detailed and help to enrich the story. I loved Lola's family especially. Her two dads are a great support system for her, and it's great to see her interactions with her involved parents, which is something we don't see too often in YA. As a bonus, it is fun to see some familiar characters from Anna turn up in this book and interesting to see how outsiders view them. The funny thing is that I thought I would miss Anna and St. Clair from the previous book, but as I became involved with this story, I was less interested in their lives and more taken with the current cast. You get the best of both worlds with the overlap of characters past and present.<br /><br />The story is set in the Bay Area and all the details written into the story lend an air of authenticity that as a former San Franciscan I enjoyed a lot. It's not Paris, but San Francisco has its charms as well. This setting is more grounded and relatable in a way.<br /><br />Stephanie Perkins again impressed me with the way she writes slow building romance, with the longing looks and sexual tension that leaps off the page. This author is great at capturing the small moments in a relationship and the feel of first love. As the boy next-door, Cricket is smart, sweet and tall, and fashion forward in his own right. Mainly he's a nice, normal guy, which can be a rarity in itself in YA books. The author takes advantage of his close proximity next door by creating cute, special opportunities for them to interact. These characters will make you melt. Fans of the first book should fall in love with this couple just as much as Anna and St. Clair.<br /><br />I wager you will enjoy this book if you like YA contemporary romance, the book Anna and the French Kiss, and books with quirky characters. It's hard to perfectly capture the magic formula that is the first book, but I found Lola and the Boy Next Door a treat to read all the same.erkins again impressed me with the way she writes slow building romance, with the longing looks and sexual tension that leaps off the page. B000035Z3K|3.0|DRACULA<br /><br />While not the first vampire movie to be released, nor the greatest, Dracula deserves a place in horror as the most defining image of the famous blood drinker.  In fact, since the 1980s, Hollywood has been trying to redefine the vampire, steering it away from the classic portrayal by Bela Lugosi, but it is a hard sell.  Lugosi took the role and made it his, and he did it so well that it is still nearly impossible to think of the word Dracula without conjuring his image.<br /><br />The 1931 Dracula follows the classic story fairly consistently.  Being the first sound version of the tale, it seems fitting that Universal would remain truer to the novel, even if just to stake its claim.  The plot should be familiar to everyone at this point, but in case you have never seen it, the story involves Renfield--a naive real-estate man--traveling to remote Eastern Europe from London to arrange for Count Dracula's transport to England.  Renfield is a modern man, and the furthest thing from his reality is the possibility of vampires.  He would rather believe the strange count is just eccentric, and that his castle is just old and spooky.  Dracula makes Renfield his slave, gets to enlightened London, and begins to feed on the unsuspecting locals.  Van Helsing is the only one who suspects Dracula's true nature, and devises a plan to destroy the beast, while saving his next victims.<br /><br />It's a great movie overall, but truth be told, it has a lot of weaknesses.  The story is that most of the people involved in it's making thought it was going to flop, that the public had no interest in vampire films, and so most of the energy put into it was weak at best.  A lot of the acting is bland, and after Dracula gets to London, it seems like everything got a lot less interesting.  The dialogue is un-interesting, the actor's delivery is dry, the sets are unexciting, and the camera work ordinary.  Then, after 30 or so minutes of boring dialogue, they go and stake Dracula and that's the end.<br /><br />But the first 20 minutes are really fantastic.  Dracula's castle is convincingly constructed and looks real.  It's a total wreck, with fallen stones and cobwebs everywhere--it reeks of decay.  How Renfield could have supposed any mortal to be able to live in this place is amazing.  The effect is creepy and adds considerably to the feeling of a present evil.  The interaction between Renfield and Dracula is great, with long silences and gazing looks, and the infamous lines Lugosi utters all lend to the atmosphere.<br /><br />Lugosi is credited with saving the film, and with good reason.  He is unbelievable in the role as Dracula.  His entire role was on the mark and convincing.  You can tell he really enjoyed the part and lived up to it.  Rumors have it Lugosi kept in character the entire filming, and I wouldn't be surprised if that were the case.  Credit should also be given to Dwight Frye who plays Renfield.  It's a very interesting character that goes from a stiff suit to a raving maniac who eats bugs all day.  When the two of them are on the screen together, it's electric.  All their scenes together are terrific, memorable.  They alone are the reasons to watch this movie, but I think it is reason enough.<br /><br />Lugosi transformed the vampire in film with this one role, and that is worth seeing, too.  Instead of playing the part as a revolting, undead devil, Lugosi added a complexity still used in the vampire role.  He is evil, his habits strange, his mannerisms suspicious, but he is also elegant, charming, and unassuming.  That's why the Lugosi vampire is so frightening, because it is like a sheep in wolves clothing.<br /><br />I feel like this movie stands as a great due to the Lugosi factor, but keep in mind that an alternate version exists, shot with all the same sets, but with Spainish actors instead.  It's a superior movie, much more interesting than the popular English version.  Its only downside is it lacks Lugosi.  Ifonly Uiversal had combined the efforts of both movies, we would have had possible the greatest horror film of all time.have had possible the greatest horror film of all time.

The film is more of a mystery than a horror flick, but seen through the eyes of the films hero, Inspector Howie, it most certainly is horror.  In this respect, it's a culturally subjective horror film, for any good pagan would watch this without so much as a flinch or a shutter.<br /><br />The movie takes place on a beautiful island off the coast of Scotland.  Warmed by the Gulf Stream Winds, it is a place known for it's prodigious fruit production.  Inspector Howie is summoned to the island to investigate the possible murder of a young girl.  It's a frustrating mystery as Howie tries to figure out what has become of her, and his inquiries inadvertently uncover that the people here are no longer Christians and have reverted back to the beliefs and practices of their pagan ancestors.<br /><br />This is greatly upsetting to Howie.  Howie is the sort of man who has made self control and reason his life's purpose.  He is a true Apollonian, a believer in the seriousness of life.  He has worked hard to become an inspector, is due to be married, is a very faithful Christian, and has maintained his sexual purity.  Counter to this are the locals, revelers in the ways of Dionysus, people who enjoy life, singing and dancing, a people for whom the ceremonies and morals of Christianity mean nothing, a people who have little need for the modern, civilized way of life.<br /><br />To make matters worse, it is spring, and so in true pagan form, the people are preparing for the islands fertility rites.  Couples make love in the open, children sing songs about procreation, phallic symbols are everywhere.  If this were not enough to turn Howie's stomach, he also has to contend with the buxom waitress in his hotel.  Not only does she flirt with him every chance she gets, but she even stoops so low as to blatantly invite the chaste inspector for a night of fun in her room, singing softly to him through the bedroom wall while dancing nude in some sort of sexual ritual.  Yeah, it's a great scene!<br /><br />Howie doesn't take the bait, nor does he ever come to terms with anything he witnesses on the island.  His horror is so great he threatens to turn their whole society inside out as punishment for their wicked ways, till he finds the pagan leader, Lord Summerisle, who politely reminds Howie that nothing illegal is taking place, and so he has no recourse.  Unless that is, he can find the body of the young girl whom Howie suspects was some sort of sacrifice.<br /><br />If you have seen the new remake of the movie, you'll have an idea of what happens next, but you should still see this movie.  It is one of the most well made films ever made, in any genre of movies.  Much of this has to do with the fantastic script and acting, but even more so with the interesting subject material and setting.  The pagan rituals, songs, fetishes and lifestyle are stunningly captured.  The film's crescendo is at the end with the May Day festive ushering in the spring, where everybody dresses up, more sexual insinuations are made, and Howie reaches his boiling point.  When all these elements are tied together, you end up with a beautiful movie that is entirely fascinating.<br /><br />Like I said before, your take on the film may be entirely subjective.  I felt like I wanted to take sides in this, and I certainly believe there is a polar duality at play here that is hard to remain nonpartisan through.  At times I sympathized with Howie, and it's hard not to.  But he comes off as a jerk mostly, and the locals increasingly become more likable.  It's not till the end that you may find your opinion of Howie really changes once everything becomes clear and his destiny unfolds.  This is where the new Wicker Man really screwed up.  The original really hits the point home and the end is breathtaking, a true work of art that words cannot justify.

It was brutally and delightfully honest, portraying the man as a multi-talented, intelligent, loving, spiritual human being with flaws like anyone else. 0670022950|4.0|I wrote my dissertation influenced by Ernst Cassirer's idea that the idea of Humanity was a galvanizing one during the Enlightenment. So, I am really glad to see how Steven Pinker provides sufficient evidence to show that the impact of the Enlightenment Humanism was overwhelmingly positive. I was a bit disappointed, however, in reading the section on it that he gives such short shrift to Jean-Jacques Rousseau. In fact, he locates him only as a Counter-Enlightenment figure.  It is true that he quarreled with philosophes who had an unthinking faith in reason and other advances in arts and letters, and many people blame him both for the terreur that followed the French Revolution as well as the later totalitarianism of the 20th century.  They both do him a grave injustice. In his La Nouvelle Heloise, which Pinker rightly emphasizes, Rousseau's characters over and over again enjoin each other to be human and respect the rights of humanity. In his Emile, he wants to raise an ordinary boy, who treats everyone, high and low, the same.  His Social Contract not only criticizes the institution of slavery, but advocates self government. Rousseau was very aware that both reason and passion, if not rightly directed, can just serve self interest. Kant was also very influenced by Rousseau, he said it was Rousseau who taught him that the vocation of philosophy was to defend the rights of humanity.  Kant also saw that reason itself is not sufficient, you need a good will to do the right thing. Denis Diderot has also often not been given his due, either. Whereas most of the other philosophes were hoping for enlightened despots, Diderot said we should be wary of those most of all as they condition people to obedience.  In my thesis, I was not interested in a fixed theory of human nature, but was more interested in how we have historically defined ourselves as both civilized and humane, and how often we have fallen short of that definition.  Pinker's voluminous testimony seems to show that despite our problems, there is still hope that we can listen to the better angels of our nature.that despite our problems, there is still hope that we can listen to the better angels of our nature.

Yeah, I'm just slowin' down to get my feet back on the ground\\".  12. \\"That Old King James\\" is an excellent way to close out the album, and I love that he has this song on here, representing his Christianity. Every single song on here could easily be a single, and they are all wonderful choices to represent Scotty.
